?
When you face objections to your faith that seem logically strong, do you dismiss them or engage deeply like the scholars? What step can you take this week to understand one such objection thoroughly?
?
The concept of 'baqa' of baqa'' shows that the ultimate reality is self-referential and non-regressive. Is your relationship with God anchored in that same self-evident certainty, or is it based on secondary things that need further justification? What needs to change?

Second Argument on Baqa': Circularity and Response
Presents the circularity argument and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the circularity objection against baqa' being an existential attribute and the response that denies real dependence between the essence and baqa'.

Core Idea
Circularity is avoided by denying that baqa' causes existence in the second moment.

This passage presents a key objection to the view that baqa' is an existential attribute: it would create a circular dependence between the essence and baqa'. The response clarifies that existence in the second moment merely coincides with baqa', not caused by it. Understanding this distinction is crucial for proper kalām reasoning about divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read and identify the two alternatives
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice that the argument presents two possible scenarios: either baqa' depends on the essence (leading to circularity) or it does not (leading to baqa' becoming the necessary existent).
The argument uses conditional reasoning (law… la…) to show both options are problematic.
2
Analyze the response
The response denies that the essence depends on baqa'. It rejects the claim that existence in the second moment is caused by baqa'. Instead, it says existence merely occurs concurrently (bimusāḥaba) with baqa'.
The key term is 'ittiḥād al-zamān' (concomitance in time), not causation.
3
Understand the concept of concommitance
The response introduces the idea that two things can exist together without one being the cause of the other. This is a crucial nuance in kalām to avoid unwanted metaphysical commitments.
Compare with the notion of 'ittiḥād' often used in discussions of attributes.
4
Reflect on theological significance
This argument and response protect the uniqueness of Allah as the necessary being. If baqa' were independent of the essence, it would rival Allah's necessity. The response preserves tawḥīd by denying real causality between attributes.
This connects to broader discussions of divine simplicity and attribute ontology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: لو احتاج البقاء على تقدير كونه وجودياً إلى الذات لزم الدور، لأن الذات محتاجة إلى البقاء أيضاً، فإن وجوده في الزمان الثاني معلل به. وإلا، أي وإن لم يحتج البقاء إلى الذات، لكان الذات محتاجاً إليه وكان مستغنياً عن الذات مع استغنائه عن غيره أيضاً، فكان البقاء هو الواجب الوجود دون الذات. والجواب: منع احتياج الذات إليه. وما قيل من أن وجوده في الزمن الثاني معلل به ممنوع، غاية ما في الباب أن وجوده فيه لا يكون إلا مع البقاء، وذلك لا يوجب أن يكون البقاء علة لوجوده فيه إذ يجوز أن يكون تحققهما على سبيل الاتفاق. وإليه الإشارة بقوله: وإن اتفق تحققهما معاً.
Second: If baqa', assuming it is existential, depends on the essence, then circularity follows, because the essence also depends on baqa', since its existence in the second moment is caused by it. Otherwise, meaning if baqa' does not depend on the essence, then the essence would be in need of it and it would be independent of the essence and also independent of other than it, so baqa' would be the necessary existence instead of the essence. The answer: the claim that the essence depends on it is denied. And what is said that its existence in the second moment is caused by it is denied; the utmost is that its existence in that moment does not occur except with baqa', and that does not necessitate that baqa' is the cause of its existence therein, since it is possible that the two occur concomitantly. This is indicated by his statement: 'even if their occurrence is together.'
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument and its response. The argument states that if baqa' (as an existential attribute) depends on the essence (dhāt), then circularity (dawr) arises because the essence also depends on baqa' (since its existence in the second moment is caused by baqa'). Alternatively, if baqa' does not depend on the essence, then the essence depends on baqa' while baqa' is independent of the essence and everything else, making baqa' the necessary being, not the essence. The response denies that the essence depends on baqa'. It further rejects that existence in the second moment is caused by baqa'; rather, existence merely occurs concurrently with baqa' (ittiḥād al-zamān). The commentator notes that this refutes the objection.

Key Terms
دور
احتياج الذات
علة
اتفاق
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The circularity objection is between two independent entities, making it impossible to resolve.
Correction: The circularity is between the essence and an attribute; the response shows that the dependence is not real but merely temporal concommitance.

Checkpoint
What does the response claim about the relationship between existence in the second moment and baqa'?
A) Baqa' is the cause of existence in the second moment
B) Existence in the second moment is identical to baqa'
C) Existence in the second moment occurs only with baqa' but not necessarily caused by it
D) Baqa' is not needed for existence in the second moment

Key Takeaway
The response to the circularity objection shows that existence and baqa' can be concomitant without causation, preserving the uniqueness of the necessary being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you ever act as if some creature or circumstance is independent of God, even while believing He is the only self-sufficient? Where specifically might you be committing the second horn of that dilemma – attributing independence to something other than Allah?
?
When you see your own continued existence (baqa') from one moment to the next, do you implicitly assume it comes from yourself? How does the truth that your existence is merely concomitant with Allah's will, not caused by any inherent attribute of yours, reduce your pride?
?
This passage denies that baqa' causes existence – even for God. Are you comfortable with the idea that no attribute of God causes His existence, but only accompanies it? What does that say about the utter simplicity and self-sufficiency of Allah – and what can you rely on other than Him?

Two Interpretations of Baqa' and Their Theological Critique
Al-Jurjani clarifies two interpretations of baqa' and critiques the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two major interpretations of the attribute of baqa' (eternal duration) and the critical analysis of the first interpretation based on the principle that existence is identical to the essence.

Core Idea
Baqa' is either continued existence or a cause – but one view contradicts the identity of existence and essence.

This lesson unpacks a subtle theological note from al-Jurjani's commentary. He clarifies that scholars interpret baqa'—God's eternal duration—in two distinct ways: either as the continuation of existence itself, or as a separate cause that sustains existence. He then warns that the first interpretation conflicts with a foundational principle of Ibn Sina's school: that existence is identical to the essence. Without careful reinterpretation, this leads to an inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Notice how the author introduces the two interpretations and immediately critiques the first.
The passage is a note (tanbīh) so it presumes prior familiarity with the attribute of baqa'.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Pinpoint the two ways baqa' is understood: (1) as continuation of existence in the second moment being additional to the essence, (2) as a meaning that causes existence in the second moment.
The first interpretation is attributed to those who deny baqa' as a real attribute (al-nāfīn).
3
Understand the commentator's critique
Al-Jurjani points out a looseness in the original text and argues that the first interpretation is incompatible with the view that existence is identical to the essence. He suggests a reinterpretation via ellipsis to avoid the problem.
Reflect on why this critique is important for Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه : إثبات البقاء قد يفسر بأن الوجود في الزمان الثاني أمر زائد على الذات يريد أن المثبتين للبقاء يفسرونه تارة باستمرار الوجود ويدعون أن الوجود في الزمان الثاني زائد على الذات، وأخرى بأنه معنى يعلل به الوجود في الزمان الثاني وأول الوجهين للنافين. قوله : ( إثبات البقاء قد يفسر إلخ) في العبارة مساهلة حيث ذكر الإثبات وفسره بالتصديق ثم لا يخفى أن زيادة الوجود في الزمان الثاني على الذات لا يتأتى على مذهب الشيخ القائل لأن الوجود عين الذات، وإذ عرفت أن الوجود في الزمان الثاني يلزم أن يكون عين الوجود في الزمان الأول، ومن البين أن العين في الزمان الأول لا يصير غيراً في الزمان الثاني اللهم إلا أن يحمل على حذف المضاف أي استمرار الوجود الحاصل في الزمان الثاني ويدعى أنه غير الوجود ولو في الزمان الثاني على أن إثبات زيادة البقاء على الوجود إنما يحصل إذا ثبت أن الاستمرار أو الوجود في الزمان الثاني زائد على أصل الوجود فكان الأهم أن يتعرض له، فتأمل.
Note: Proving baqa' is interpreted as existence in the second moment being additional to the essence. He means that those who affirm baqa' interpret it sometimes as the continuation of existence and claim that existence in the second moment is additional to the essence, and other times as a meaning that causes existence in the second moment. The first of the two interpretations is for the deniers. His statement: 'Proving baqa' may be interpreted etc.' contains a looseness in the expression where he mentions proof (ithbāt) and interprets it as affirmation (taṣdīq). It is clear that the increase of existence in the second moment over the essence is not feasible according to the doctrine of the Master (al-Shaykh) because existence is identical to the essence. You know that existence in the second moment must be identical to existence in the first moment, and it is obvious that the identical in the first moment does not become something else in the second moment. Unless it is understood as ellipsis, meaning the continuation of existence occurring in the second moment, and it is claimed that it is other than existence, even if only in the second moment. However, proving the increase of baqa' over existence only holds if it is established that the continuation or existence in the second moment is additional to the original existence. So it was important to address it. Reflect.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a note (tanbīh) that clarifies two interpretations of proving baqa'. The first interpretation: baqa' is the continuation of existence, and existence in the second moment is additional to the essence. The second: baqa' is a meaning that causes existence in the second moment. The first interpretation is attributed to the deniers (al-nāfīn). The commentator (al-Jurjani) then critiques the expression of the base text, noting a looseness (musāhala) in using 'ithbāt' (proof) to mean 'taṣdīq' (affirmation). He points out that positing an increase of existence in the second moment over the essence is inconsistent with the doctrine of al-Shaykh (al-Sadr al-Shīrāzī or al-Shaykh al-Raʾīs Ibn Sīnā) because existence is identical to the essence. He explains that existence in the second moment must be identical to existence in the first moment, so it cannot become something else. However, if we interpret baqa' as the continuation of existence (by ellipsis), then it might be considered additional. The commentator concludes that this requires careful reflection (tāammul).

Key Terms
إثبات البقاء
استمرار الوجود
زيادة على الذات
تعليل
مذهب الشيخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the two interpretations are merely different wordings of the same idea, or that the first interpretation is unproblematic.
Correction: The two interpretations are distinct: one is ontological (continued existence as an added attribute), the other is causal (baqa' as a cause of existence). The first interpretation faces a serious logical inconsistency with the principle that existence is identical to the essence, as noted by al-Jurjani.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why is the first interpretation problematic?
A) It implies that baqa' is not a real attribute
B) It leads to circularity
C) It is incompatible with the doctrine that existence is identical to the essence
D) It requires an infinite regress

Key Takeaway
The first interpretation of baqa' as continued existence added to the essence is problematic because it contradicts the identity of existence and essence, requiring reinterpretation as a cause or via ellipsis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah's attributes like al-Baqi (the Ever-Enduring), do you unconsciously picture them as add-ons to His essence? How does this passage's insistence on the identity of existence and essence challenge you to purify your concept of Allah?
?
The critique here rests on a subtle philosophical point. Are you willing to accept that some truths about Allah may require you to abandon intuitive notions like 'adding' attributes? What does that reveal about your intellectual humility before divine revelation?
?
This passage forces you to choose between two interpretations of permanence: as a mere continuation or as a causal act. Which interpretation makes you feel closer to Allah? Why? And what does your preference say about how you relate to His eternal nature?

Proof and Objection on Baqa'
Commentary on proof of baqa' and answering the vicious circle objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof for God's attribute of baqa' (eternal subsistence), the objection that it leads to a vicious circle, and the answer that distinguishes between essential need and concurrent existence.

Core Idea
Baqa' is not a cause but a concurrent condition of existence in the second moment.

This passage tackles a subtle point in the proof of baqa' (eternal subsistence) as an attribute of God. The commentator, al-Sharif al-Jurjani, navigates the objection that affirming baqa' as a cause for continued existence leads to a vicious circle. He clarifies that the existence in the second moment does not require baqa' as a cause; rather, they occur concurrently, avoiding circularity while preserving the necessary existence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main argument
Read the first part of the passage where the author restates that baqa' is necessary existence due to absolute independence. Note the claim that baqa' is 'the necessary existence' because of absolute independence.
This sets up the proof.
2
Understand the objection
The objection is that if baqa' is a cause for existence in the second moment, then a vicious circle arises. Look for the phrase 'دور' and grasp the concern that baqa' would depend on existence and vice versa.
The circle is the key issue.
3
Grasp the answer
The answer denies the need of the essence for baqa' and explains that existence and baqa' can be concurrent (اتفاق). Pay attention to the pronoun clarification: 'their occurrence' refers to baqa' and existence, not essence and baqa'.
This avoids the circle.
4
Note the two interpretations
The text mentions two interpretations of baqa': as a continuation of existence (استمرار) or as a cause (علة) for existence in the second moment. Understand the difference and which one the negators target.
The first interpretation is rejected as leading to circularity; the second is defended.

The Fourth Observatory - The Eighth Purpose: Concerning that He, exalted, is an attribute. (From the essence) along with His being independent of others also, so (al-baqa') is the necessary existence because it is absolute independence (not the essence). And the answer: denial of the need of the essence for it. And what is said that His existence in the second moment is caused by it is rejected. The most that is in the matter is that His existence in it is only with baqa', but that does not necessitate that baqa' is the cause of His existence in it, for it is possible that both occur concurrently, and that is indicated by his saying: 'And if their occurrence coincides.' (Note: The affirmation of baqa' may be interpreted as the existence in the second moment being additional to the essence. He means that those who affirm baqa' interpret it sometimes as the continuation of existence and claim that the existence in the second moment is additional to the essence, and sometimes as a meaning that causes the existence in the second moment. And the first of the two aspects for the negators with respect to their existence in the second moment, so there is no vicious circle, because we say: Rather, the claim is that the subsistence of baqa' requires the existence of the essence in the second moment, so reflect.) His statement: (And the answer: denial of the need of the essence for it. His statement: (The affirmation of baqa' may be interpreted, etc.) There is leniency in the expression, for he mentioned affirmation (al-ithbat) and interpreted it as belief (al-tasdiq). Then it is not hidden that the increase of existence in the second moment over the essence is not sustainable according to the doctrine of the Master who says that existence is identical to the essence. And since you know that existence in the second moment must be identical to existence in the first moment, and it is evident that the same in the first moment does not become other in the second moment, unless one assumes deletion of the genitive, i.e., the continuation of existence occurring in the second moment, and claims that it is other than existence, even in the second moment; while affirming the increase of baqa' over existence only occurs if it is established that the continuation or existence in the second moment is additional to the original existence. So it was more important to address that. Reflect.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the proof of baqa' (eternal subsistence) as an attribute of God, followed by objections and answers. The commentator begins by restating the argument that since God is absolutely independent (ghani mutlaq), baqa' is the necessary existence, not the essence itself. The answer to the objection is that the essence does not need baqa' for its existence in the second moment; it is possible that both existence and baqa' occur concurrently without causation. Then a note (tanbih) explains two interpretations of baqa' by its proponents: (1) as continuation of existence, claiming existence in the second moment is additional to the essence, and (2) as a meaning that causes existence in the second moment. The first interpretation is rejected by negators as leading to a vicious circle. The commentator then explains that the pronoun in 'their occurrence' refers to baqa' and existence in the second moment, not the essence and baqa', so the objection of circularity is avoided. Further, the denial of causation is argued to be a persuasive (ilzami) rather than veridical (tahqiqi) proof. The objection that this makes the Necessary dependent on something other than itself is refuted by appealing to the impossibility of existence depending on something else. Finally, the commentator notes the difficulty in affirming baqa' as an additional attribute within the Ash'ari doctrine that existence is identical to the essence, but suggests that one could interpret it as continuation of existence in the second moment.

Correction: The passage shows that the denial is based on the possibility of concurrence, avoiding the circle, and that the objection is answered by distinguishing between essence and existence in the second moment.

The Two Faces of Negation of Baqa'
Explanation of two ways to negate baqa' and a subsequent critical comment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two logical ways to negate baqa' (divine endurance) and the subtle debate about whether the first face negates one or both meanings of baqa'.

Core Idea
Baqa' can be negated in two ways, each with different logical consequences.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif explores two interpretations (wajh) for denying baqa' – the attribute of divine endurance. The author first distinguishes two meanings of baqa' – as continuation of existence and as a cause for existence in the next moment – then shows how each face of negation targets a specific meaning. A subsequent gloss raises a critical objection that forces us to reconsider what the first face actually accomplishes. Understanding this nuance is essential for grasping the kalam debate on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic and translation to grasp the author's two faces and the comment. Pay attention to the distinction between the two meanings of baqa'.
The passage has two main parts: the explanation of the two faces and the subsequent commentary (qawl).
2
Identify the two meanings of baqa'
Notice that baqa' is used in two senses: (1) continuation of existence (istimrar al-wujud) and (2) a causal entity that ensures existence in the next moment. Distinguishing these is key to understanding the faces.
The first meaning is about duration; the second is about causation.
3
Map the two faces of negation
The first face negates the first meaning (continuation) but not necessarily the second. The second face negates the second meaning (causal aspect) without negating the first. Observe how the text avoids a vicious circle (dawr).
The second face's reasoning: continuity of existence does not force the essence to need a separate continuation attribute.
4
Engage with the commentary
The gloss challenges the first face, arguing that if baqa' is a cause, its endurance is needed for subsequent moments. This would mean the first face actually negates both meanings, unless we consider a 'preparer' (mu'idd) specific to temporal events.
This shows the subtlety: the first face might not be as clean as initially thought.
5
Reflect on the author's resolution
The passage concludes with the author's preferred view: the essence of baqa' causes both the essence's existence and its own existence in the second moment, so no further baqa' is needed. This resolves the difficulty raised in the commentary.
The final 'so reflect' (fa-ta'ammal) invites deep contemplation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ينفي ( المعني ( الأول : ( من معنى البقاء لأن الاستمرار إذا لم يكن باقياً لم يكن الوجود مستمراً، ولا ينفي الثاني لأن البقاء إذا كان أمراً يعلل به الوجود في الزمان الثاني لا يلزم أن يكون له بقاء آخر ، ( و ) الوجه ( الثاني : ( ينفي المعنى ( الثاني ) دون الأول إذ لا يلزم من استمرار الوجود وكونه زائداً على الذات احتياج الذات في وجوده إلى البقاء الذي هو الاستمرار فلا يلزم الدور.
قوله : (لأن الاستمرار إذا لم يكن باقياً إلخ) فيه بحث لأن أصل الاستمرار كاف لاستمرار الوجود في الزمان الثاني، وأما بقاء الاستمرار فهو لاستمرار الوجود في الزمان الثالث ثم لا شك أن أصل الأمر الذي علل به الوجود في الزمان الثاني إنما يكفي للوجود فيه، وأما للوجود في الزمان الثالث فلا يكفي بل لا بد من بقائه لأنه علة وانعدام العلة يوجب انعدام المعلول وحينئذ لا يبقى فرق بين معنى البقاء في أن الوجه الأول ينفي كليهما، اللهم إلا أن يقال : إن معنى الأمر الذي علل به الوجود يعم المعد ويجوز انعدام المعد لكن هذا مختص بالحوادث، والأظهر أن مراده أن ذات البقاء أمر يعلل به وجود الذات ووجود نفسه في الزمان الثاني ولا يحتاج إلى بقاء آخر بهذا المعنى فتأمل .
(It negates) the (first meaning) of the meaning of baqa', because continuation (al-istimrar) if it is not enduring (baqiyan) then existence would not be continuous, and it does not negate the second, because if baqa' is a thing by which existence in the second moment is caused, it does not necessitate that it has another baqa'. (And) the (second) face negates the (second) meaning without the first, because from the continuity of existence and its being additional to the essence, it does not necessitate the need of the essence in its existence for the baqa' which is the continuation, so there is no vicious circle.
His statement: (Because if continuation is not enduring, etc.) There is a discussion in it, because the original continuation is sufficient for the continuation of existence in the second moment. As for the endurance of continuation, it is for the continuation of existence in the third moment. Then there is no doubt that the original thing by which existence in the second moment is caused is sufficient for existence in it. As for existence in the third moment, it is not sufficient; rather, its endurance is necessary because it is a cause, and the nonexistence of the cause necessitates the nonexistence of the effect. Then there is no difference left between the meaning of baqa' in that the first face negates both, unless it is said: The meaning of the thing by which existence is caused includes the preparer (al-mu'idd) and it is permissible for the preparer to cease to exist, but this is specific to temporal events (al-hawadith). The more apparent is that his intention is that the essence of baqa' is a thing that causes the existence of the essence and its own existence in the second moment, and it does not need another baqa' in this sense. So reflect.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage first explains two faces (two ways) of negating baqa'. The first face negates the first meaning of baqa' (continuation of existence) because if the continuation itself were not enduring, then existence would not be continuous; but it does not negate the second meaning (baqa' as a cause). The second face negates the second meaning without negating the first, because the continuity of existence and its being additional to the essence do not require the essence to need baqa' as continuation, so no vicious circle. Then a commentary (qawl) discusses the phrase 'because if continuation is not enduring...' It points out that the original continuation is sufficient for existence in the second moment; the endurance of continuation is needed for the third moment. Thus, the first face actually negates both meanings of baqa' if understood in a certain way, unless one considers that the cause includes a 'preparer' (mu'idd) which may cease to exist—but that is specific to temporal events. The more apparent interpretation is that baqa' itself causes both the existence of the essence and its own existence in the second moment, so it does not require another baqa'.

Key Takeaway
The negation of baqa' can be understood in two ways, each targeting a different meaning, and the subsequent commentary reveals a subtle interplay between the temporal logic of causation and the eternal nature of divine attributes.

The passage shows that even scholars must carefully distinguish meanings to avoid contradictions in understanding Allah. The Consensus: Qidam as a Negative Attribute
States the consensus that God is eternal by Himself, not by an additional attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the mainstream Ash'ari view considers qidam (eternity) to be a negative attribute, not a positive existential attribute added to the divine essence.

Core Idea
Qidam is not a thing added to God; He is eternal by His very nature.

This lesson presents the foundational consensus among Sunni scholars that God's eternity is not an extra quality but a necessary aspect of His self. The term qidam is often misunderstood as a positive attribute, but the mainstream tradition emphasizes that it is a denial of a beginning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Notice the phrase 'muttāfiqīn ʿalā annahu qadīm bi-nafsihī lā bi-qidam wujūdī'.
The phrase 'wa-iḥālata al-jumhūr' may be a corruption of 'wa-ijmāʿ al-jumhūr' (consensus of the mainstream).
2
Identify the consensus statement
Locate the explicit statement that God is eternal by Himself, not by an additional attribute. This is the core of the lesson.
3
Reflect on the meaning of negation
Consider what it means for qidam to be a 'salbi' (negative) attribute. Think about how this differs from attributes like power or knowledge.
This connects to the learning objective.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصفة ( الثانية : القدم وإحالة الجمهور متفقين على أنه قديم بنفسه لا بقدم وجودي (زائد) على ذاته، وأثبته ابن سعيد ) من الأشاعرة ( ودليله ) على كونه صفة موجودة زائدة ( ما مر في البقاء)، وتصويره هاهنا أن يقال : القديم قد يطلق على المتقدم بالوجود إذا تطاول عليه الأمد، ومنه قوله تعالى : كالعرجون القديم ﴾ [يس: ٣٩] ، والجسم لا يوصف بهذا القدم في أول زمان حدوثه بل ،بعده فقد تجدد له القدم بعدما لم يكن فيكون موجوداً زائداً على الذات فكذا القدم الذي هو التقدم بلا نهاية لا بمجرد مدة متطاولة ( بإبطاله ) أي مر مع إبطاله، فلا حاجة إلى إعادة شيء منهما وحمل ما مر على الوجهين السابقين مما لا وجه لصحته (و) الذي ( يخصصه ) أي يختص بإبطاله ( أنه إن أراد به ) أي بالقدم ( أنه لا أول له فسلبي فلا يتصور كونه وجوديا ( أو أنه صفة لأجلها لا يختص الباري سبحانه وتعالى ( بحيز كما فسره أي كما فسر كلام ابن سعيد بذلك ( الشيخ أبو إسحاق الإسفرايني ) فإنه قال : معنى كلامه أنه تعالى مختص بمعنى لأجله ثبت وجودة لا في حيز كما أن المتحيز يختص بمعنى لأجله كان متحيزاً، ولا يخفى عليك أن هذا التفسير بعيد جداً عن دلالة زيادة القدم عليه ( فكذلك ) يكون ما
قوله : ( كالعرجون القديم العرجون عود العرق ما بين شماريخه إلى منبته من النخلة. قوله : ( فكذا القدم الذي هو التقدم بلا (نهاية فيه منع للقطع بتغاير المفهومين ودليل الوجودية غير قائم لنوع أحدهما ولو سلم الاتحاد في النوع فوجودية فرد من نوع لا يستلزم وجودية فرد آخر.
قوله : ( مما لا وجه لصحته لأنهما للنافين لا للمثبتين .
The (second) attribute: al-qidam (eternity/past eternity). And the consensus of the mainstream is that He is eternal by Himself, not by an existential qidam (additional) to His essence. Ibn Sa'id (one of the Ash'aris) affirmed it, and his proof for its being an existential attribute additional (to the essence) is what has preceded on baqa'. Its representation here is that it is said: 'al-qadim' may be applied to that which precedes in existence when time extends over it, and from that is His saying: 'Like the old dry palm branch' (Q 36:39). A body is not described with this qidam in the first moment of its origination, but after it, so qidam has been newly added for it after it was not, so it becomes an existent additional to the essence. Similarly, qidam which is precedence without end, not merely an extended period, (its refutation) i.e., it has passed with its refutation, so there is no need to repeat any of it. And applying what has passed to the two previous aspects has no basis for correctness. (And) that which (specializes it) i.e., is specific to its refutation: that if he means by it (i.e., by qidam) that it has no beginning, then it is a negation, so it cannot be conceived as existential; or that it is an attribute for which the Most High and Exalted is not characterized by a spatial location, as interpreted—i.e., as Ibn Sa'id's statement was interpreted by Shaykh Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini, for he said: The meaning of his statement is that He is characterized by a meaning due to which His existence was established not in a spatial location, just as the spatially characterized is characterized by a meaning due to which it is spatially characterized. And it is not hidden from you that this interpretation is very far from indicating the increase of qidam upon Him. (So similarly) it is...
His statement: (Like the old dry palm branch) al-'urjun is the stalk of the raceme, from its spathes to its origin from the palm tree. His statement: (So similarly qidam which is precedence without (end) contains a denial of the certainty of the difference of the two concepts, and the proof of existentiality is not established for either type; even if unity in type is granted, the existentiality of an individual of a type does not necessitate the existentiality of another individual.
His statement: (What has no basis for correctness) because they are for the negators, not for the affirmers.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage discusses the attribute of qidam (eternity/past eternity). It states the consensus of the mainstream that God is eternal by Himself, not by an additional existential attribute of qidam. However, Ibn Sa'id (an Ash'ari) affirmed it as an additional attribute, analogizing it to baqa' with the same proof. The explanation of his proof is that 'qadim' can be applied to something that precedes in existence over time, like a dry palm branch, so a body that is not qadim at its origin becomes qadim after time passes, thus qidam is an additional existent. Then the commentator refutes this: if qidam means 'no beginning', it is a negation (salbi) and cannot be existential; if it is interpreted as an attribute due to which God is not characterized by spatial location (as al-Isfara'ini interpreted Ibn Sa'id), that interpretation is far from indicating a positive increase. Furthermore, the commentator discusses a gloss on 'kal'urjun al-qadim' (like the old dry palm branch), explaining its lexical meaning, and then notes that the comparison of qidam as precedence without end to that temporal qidam is invalid because the two concepts differ. Even if they were the same type, the existentiality of one individual does not entail the existentiality of another. Finally, a comment says that the two aspects (the two faces) mentioned earlier regarding baqa' are for negators, not for affirmers.

Key Terms
القدم
القديم
الصفة السلبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Qidam is an existential attribute added to God's essence, like His power or knowledge.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that God is eternal by Himself, not by an additional existential qidam. Thus qidam is not a positive quality but a negation of a beginning.

Checkpoint
According to the mainstream view presented, how is God described with respect to qidam?
A) He has an eternal attribute added to His essence.
B) He is eternal by Himself, without an additional attribute.
C) He is eternal only through His actions.
D) He is not eternal at all.

Key Takeaway
The mainstream Ash'ari view is that God's eternity is not an extra attribute; He is eternal by His own essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's eternity is not an additional attribute but His very essence, what does that demand from you in terms of relying on Him? Does your heart truly depend on a God who is eternal by Himself, or do you still seek security in created things that have a beginning?
?
The consensus indicates that God has no beginning. How does this reality challenge the way you live each day as if the world around you will last forever? What would change if you truly lived with the awareness that only God is eternal?
?
The mainstream view denies an existential qidam added to God's essence, meaning His attributes are not separate from His reality. Does your worship reflect a belief in a God whose attributes are intrinsic to Him, or do you unknowingly compartmentalize His attributes in a way that diminishes His unity?

Ibn Sa'id's Argument for Positive Qidam and Its Refutation
Presents Ibn Sa'id's affirmation of qidam as a positive attribute and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sa'id's argument that qidam is a positive existential attribute and how it is refuted by showing that qidam either means negation or is an attribute unrelated to spatial location.

Core Idea
Ibn Sa'id's proof for a positive qidam fails because qidam for God means 'no beginning,' which is a negation.

This lesson explores a minority view within Ash'arism that attempted to treat qidam as an existential attribute. Ibn Sa'id's argument from language and analogy is presented, followed by a powerful refutation that exposes the category error.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. It includes Ibn Sa'id's proof and the refutation.
Note the use of the Quranic verse 'kal-ʿurjūni al-qadīm' (like the old dry palm branch).
2
Identify Ibn Sa'id's proof
Find how he argues that qidam is existential: by analogy with baqā' and by applying the term 'qadīm' to temporal things.
3
Understand the two refutation points
The refutation offers two interpretations: (1) if qidam means no beginning, it is a negation; (2) if it means an attribute for not being in a location, that interpretation is far-fetched.
Also note the comment that the analogy does not hold because the concepts differ.
4
Reflect on the outcome
Consider why the proof fails and what this means for the nature of qidam as a negative attribute.
This connects to the learning objective.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Two Meanings of Abiding
Presents the two meanings and initial argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two meanings of the attribute of abiding (al-Baqā') and the initial argument that negates one without the other.

Core Idea
Abiding has two meanings: continuance and causal presence; each is addressed differently.

Sanusi begins by distinguishing two meanings of the divine attribute of abiding: continuance (istimrār) and that which causes existence in the second time. He then argues that the first meaning is negated by the attribute itself, while the second meaning is not, because if abiding were that cause, it would not need another abiding. This distinction is crucial to avoid infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage that defines the two meanings and presents the two aspects of the argument. Focus on the structure: first aspect negates the first meaning, second aspect negates the second.
Notice that the argument uses conditional reasoning.
2
Identify the two meanings
From the first sentence, identify the two meanings of abiding: (1) continuance (al-istimrār) and (2) that which causes existence in the second time. Understand how they differ.
3
Understand the first aspect
Analyze why the first meaning is negated: if continuance were not abiding, existence would not be continuous. This shows that abiding is necessary for continuity.
This argument highlights the interdependence of continuance and abiding.
4
Understand the second aspect
Analyze why the second meaning is not negated: if abiding is the cause of existence in the second time, it does not require another abiding for itself, thus no circularity.
This avoids an infinite regress of abidings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثامن : في أنه تعالى صفة ينفي المعنى الأول من معنى البقاء لأن الاستمرار إذا لم يكن باقياً لم يكن الوجود مستمراً، ولا ينفي الثاني لأن البقاء إذا كان أمراً يعلل به الوجود في الزمان الثاني لا يلزم أن يكون له بقاء آخر، والوجه الثاني: ينفي المعنى الثاني دون الأول إذ لا يلزم من استمرار الوجود وكونه زائداً على الذات احتياج الذات في وجوده إلى البقاء الذي هو الاستمرار فلا يلزم الدور. قوله : (لأن الاستمرار إذا لم يكن باقياً إلخ) فيه بحث لأن أصل الاستمرار كاف لاستمرار الوجود في الزمان الثاني، وأما بقاء الاستمرار فهو لاستمرار الوجود في الزمان الثالث ثم لا شك أن أصل الأمر الذي علل به الوجود في الزمان الثاني إنما يكفي للوجود فيه، وأما للوجود في الزمان الثالث فلا يكفي بل لا بد من بقائه لأنه علة وانعدام العلة يوجب انعدام المعلول وحينئذ لا يبقى فرق بين معنى البقاء في أن الوجه الأول ينفي كليهما، اللهم إلا أن يقال : إن معنى الأمر الذي علل به الوجود يعم المعد ويجوز انعدام المعد لكن هذا مختص بالحوادث، والأظهر أن مراده أن ذات البقاء أمر يعلل به وجود الذات ووجود نفسه في الزمان الثاني ولا يحتاج إلى بقاء آخر بهذا المعنى فتأمل .
This is the fourth observatory - the eighth objective: concerning that He, exalted, is an attribute. He negates the first meaning from the meaning of abiding (al-baqā') because if continuance (al-istimrār) is not abiding, existence would not be continuous, and He does not negate the second because if abiding is something by which existence is caused in the second time, it does not necessitate that it has another abiding. And the second aspect: it negates the second meaning without the first, because from the continuance of existence and its being additional to the essence, it does not necessitate that the essence in its existence needs the abiding which is continuance, so there is no circularity (al-dawr). His saying: 'Because if continuance is not abiding, etc.' – there is an investigation in it, because the origin of continuance is sufficient for the continuance of existence in the second time. As for the abiding of continuance, it is for the continuance of existence in the third time. Then there is no doubt that the origin of the thing by which existence is caused in the second time is sufficient for existence in it, but for existence in the third time it is not sufficient; rather, its abiding is necessary because it is a cause, and the non-existence of the cause necessitates the non-existence of the effect. And in that case, there remains no difference between the two meanings of abiding in that the first aspect negates both of them, unless it is said that the meaning of the thing by which existence is caused includes that which prepares (al-mu'idd), and the non-existence of the preparer is permissible, but this is specific to contingent things. And the most apparent is that his intent is that the essence of abiding is something by which the existence of the essence and its own existence in the second time are caused, and it does not need another abiding in this sense, so consider.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is a definition and argument concerning the attribute of abiding (al-baqā') and its two meanings. It begins by stating that the attribute negates the first meaning—if continuance were not abiding, existence would not be continuous—so it shows that abiding is necessary for the continuity of existence. It then explains that the second meaning is not negated because if abiding is that which causes existence in the second moment, it does not require another abiding for itself. The second aspect negates the second meaning only, arguing that the continuance of existence, being additional to the essence, does not require that the essence depends on that continuance as abiding, thus avoiding circularity. The commentator then discusses a scholarly investigation: the continuance of existence in the second moment is sufficient, but for the third moment, the continuance itself must persist, otherwise the cause would cease and the effect would cease. This would blur the distinction between the two meanings, unless one considers that the cause includes both the essential cause and the preparation, but that is specific to contingents. The intended meaning is that the essence of abiding is that which causes the existence of the essence and itself in the second moment without needing another abiding.

Key Terms
بقاء (baqā')
استمرار (istimrār)
دور (dawr)
علة (ʿilla)
معلول (maʿlūl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The two meanings of abiding are identical or that both are negated by the same argument.
Correction: The passage clearly distinguishes them: the first meaning (continuance) is negated by the necessity of continuity, while the second meaning (causal presence) is not negated because it does not require another abiding.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the second aspect of the argument negate only the second meaning of abiding?
A) Because the first meaning is essential.
B) Because continuance of existence does not require that the essence needs that continuance as abiding.
C) Because the first meaning is negated by the first aspect.
D) Because abiding is not additional.

Key Takeaway
The attribute of abiding has two distinct meanings, and the argument shows that each is handled differently to avoid circularity and infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own journey of faith, have you ever felt that your connection to Allah (ﷻ) needs to be continuously renewed by some external cause, or are you resting on the certainty that Allah's essence is sufficient? How does the argument about abiding challenge you to depend solely on Him without needing a chain of causes?
?
The passage avoids circularity by distinguishing two meanings of abiding. Are there areas in your life where you are trapped in circular thinking—expecting something to come from something that depends on you? How can this lesson's logic free you to rely on the Self-Sufficient (al-Ṣamad)?
?
Reflect on the second meaning of abiding as causal presence. Is your relationship with Allah (ﷻ) merely one of continuance (habitual rituals) or is it a genuine causal presence that sustains you moment by moment? What would change if you treated each moment as directly caused by Him without relying on past moments?

The Scholarly Investigation on Abiding and Causality
Investigates causality and continuity of abiding

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the deeper investigation into whether the cause of existence in the second time suffices for the third, and how the essence of abiding is understood to avoid circularity.

Core Idea
The cause of existence must itself abide to cause further existence; otherwise, an infinite regress arises.

After presenting the initial distinction, Sanusi delves into a scholarly investigation. He examines whether the continuance of existence in the second moment is sufficient for the third, and discusses the role of the preparer (mu'idd) versus the cause (illa). The resolution is that the essence of abiding causes both the essence's existence and its own persistence without needing another abiding, thus avoiding circularity.

Guided Path
1
Read the investigation passage
Read the Arabic passage that begins with 'قوله : (لأن الاستمرار إذا لم يكن باقياً إلخ)' and continues to the end. Notice the questioning tone and the counterarguments.
This section is a deeper reflection on the initial argument.
2
Understand the problem of sufficiency
The passage questions whether the origin of continuance (asl al-istimrār) suffices for existence in the second time. It argues that for the third time, abiding is needed, otherwise the cause would cease and the effect would not persist.
This leads to a potential blurring of the distinction between the two meanings.
3
Analyze the role of the preparer
The passage mentions that if the cause is considered a preparer (mu'idd), its non-existence may be permissible, but this is specific to contingent things (hawādith). This distinction helps maintain the difference between the two meanings.
This nuance is crucial for divine attributes.
4
Grasp the final interpretation
The passage concludes that the essence of abiding (dhat al-baqā') causes the existence of the essence and its own existence in the second time without needing another abiding. This resolves the apparent tension.
'So consider' (tāammal) indicates the reader should reflect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثامن : في أنه تعالى صفة ينفي المعنى الأول من معنى البقاء لأن الاستمرار إذا لم يكن باقياً لم يكن الوجود مستمراً، ولا ينفي الثاني لأن البقاء إذا كان أمراً يعلل به الوجود في الزمان الثاني لا يلزم أن يكون له بقاء آخر، والوجه الثاني: ينفي المعنى الثاني دون الأول إذ لا يلزم من استمرار الوجود وكونه زائداً على الذات احتياج الذات في وجوده إلى البقاء الذي هو الاستمرار فلا يلزم الدور. قوله : (لأن الاستمرار إذا لم يكن باقياً إلخ) فيه بحث لأن أصل الاستمرار كاف لاستمرار الوجود في الزمان الثاني، وأما بقاء الاستمرار فهو لاستمرار الوجود في الزمان الثالث ثم لا شك أن أصل الأمر الذي علل به الوجود في الزمان الثاني إنما يكفي للوجود فيه، وأما للوجود في الزمان الثالث فلا يكفي بل لا بد من بقائه لأنه علة وانعدام العلة يوجب انعدام المعلول وحينئذ لا يبقى فرق بين معنى البقاء في أن الوجه الأول ينفي كليهما، اللهم إلا أن يقال : إن معنى الأمر الذي علل به الوجود يعم المعد ويجوز انعدام المعد لكن هذا مختص بالحوادث، والأظهر أن مراده أن ذات البقاء أمر يعلل به وجود الذات ووجود نفسه في الزمان الثاني ولا يحتاج إلى بقاء آخر بهذا المعنى فتأمل .
This is the fourth observatory - the eighth objective: concerning that He, exalted, is an attribute. He negates the first meaning from the meaning of abiding (al-baqā') because if continuance (al-istimrār) is not abiding, existence would not be continuous, and He does not negate the second because if abiding is something by which existence is caused in the second time, it does not necessitate that it has another abiding. And the second aspect: it negates the second meaning without the first, because from the continuance of existence and its being additional to the essence, it does not necessitate that the essence in its existence needs the abiding which is continuance, so there is no circularity (al-dawr). His saying: 'Because if continuance is not abiding, etc.' – there is an investigation in it, because the origin of continuance is sufficient for the continuance of existence in the second time. As for the abiding of continuance, it is for the continuance of existence in the third time. Then there is no doubt that the origin of the thing by which existence is caused in the second time is sufficient for existence in it, but for existence in the third time it is not sufficient; rather, its abiding is necessary because it is a cause, and the non-existence of the cause necessitates the non-existence of the effect. And in that case, there remains no difference between the two meanings of abiding in that the first aspect negates both of them, unless it is said that the meaning of the thing by which existence is caused includes that which prepares (al-mu'idd), and the non-existence of the preparer is permissible, but this is specific to contingent things. And the most apparent is that his intent is that the essence of abiding is something by which the existence of the essence and its own existence in the second time are caused, and it does not need another abiding in this sense, so consider.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is a definition and argument concerning the attribute of abiding (al-baqā') and its two meanings. It begins by stating that the attribute negates the first meaning—if continuance were not abiding, existence would not be continuous—so it shows that abiding is necessary for the continuity of existence. It then explains that the second meaning is not negated because if abiding is that which causes existence in the second moment, it does not require another abiding for itself. The second aspect negates the second meaning only, arguing that the continuance of existence, being additional to the essence, does not require that the essence depends on that continuance as abiding, thus avoiding circularity. The commentator then discusses a scholarly investigation: the continuance of existence in the second moment is sufficient, but for the third moment, the continuance itself must persist, otherwise the cause would cease and the effect would cease. This would blur the distinction between the two meanings, unless one considers that the cause includes both the essential cause and the preparation, but that is specific to contingents. The intended meaning is that the essence of abiding is that which causes the existence of the essence and itself in the second moment without needing another abiding.

Key Terms
بقاء (baqā')
استمرار (istimrār)
دور (dawr)
علة (ʿilla)
معلول (maʿlūl)
معد (muʿidd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the argument leads to an infinite regress of abidings, making the concept of a self-sufficient divine attribute impossible.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the essence of abiding is self-causing in the second time and does not require another abiding, thus breaking the regress.

Checkpoint
According to the scholarly investigation, what is the consequence if the origin of continuance is not sufficient for the third time?
A) The distinction between the two meanings becomes blurred.
B) The attribute of abiding is negated entirely.
C) A new attribute is needed.
D) The essence of abiding requires another abiding.

Key Takeaway
The essence of abiding is self-sufficient: it causes existence in the second time without needing another abiding, thus preventing circularity and preserving the distinction between its two meanings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own reliance on Allah, do you ever treat His provisions (like health or wealth) as essential causes rather than mere preparations? How does the lesson's distinction between the cause and the preparer challenge you to purify your tawheed?
?
The essence of abiding is self-sufficient and does not need another to sustain it. In what area of your life are you depending on something other than Allah to keep you going—perhaps your own efforts, a relationship, or a habit? What will you do this week to shift that dependence back to the Self-Sufficient?
?
This passage resolves an infinite regress by pointing to a self-causing cause. Have you ever despaired thinking your faith or repentance needed multiple layers of human effort to be accepted? How can the truth that Allah's essence of abiding is the ultimate cause free you from that burden and move you to sincere reliance on Him alone?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
5 min
Pre-eternity is Not an Additional Attribute
Refutes the view that pre-eternity is an added attribute, showing it is negative or mental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why pre-eternity (al-qidam) cannot be an additional positive attribute and how it is interpreted as a negative concept or a mental consideration.

Core Idea
Pre-eternity is either negative or mental, not a positive attribute added to God's essence.

Sanusi presents the consensus that God is pre-eternal by Himself, then refutes the claim that pre-eternity is an additional attribute. The key move is to show that the meaning of 'pre-eternity' either refers to 'no beginning' (negative) or to 'not in a place' (which is already covered by other attributes). This lesson walks through these arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the consensus and Ibn Sa'id's view
Begin by reading the Arabic passage that states the majority agree God is pre-eternal by Himself, not by an additional attribute. Then note the counterview of Ibn Sa'id.
The passage uses 'وإحالة الجمهور' which may be read as 'wa-ahāla al-jumhūr' meaning 'the majority considered impossible'.
2
Understand the first refutation: pre-eternity as 'no beginning'
The author argues that if pre-eternity means 'no beginning', then it is a negative concept (salbi), not a positive existence. Thus it cannot be an additional attribute.
Pay attention to the distinction between positive (wujūdī) and negative (salbī) attributes.
3
Understand the second refutation: pre-eternity as 'not in a place'
If pre-eternity is interpreted as the attribute that excludes spatiality (as al-Isfarayini suggested), it is still not additional because it merely negates something.
The author says this interpretation is 'very far from the indication' of additional pre-eternity.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how both interpretations lead to the same conclusion: pre-eternity is not an additional positive attribute. This is a classic Ash'ari position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصفة الثانية : القدم وإحالة الجمهور متفقين على أنه قديم بنفسه لا بقدم وجودي زائد على ذاته، وأثبته ابن سعيد من الأشاعرة ودليله على كونه صفة موجودة زائدة ما مر في البقاء، وتصويره هاهنا أن يقال : القديم قد يطلق على المتقدم بالوجود إذا تطاول عليه الأمد، ومنه قوله تعالى : {كالعرجون القديم} [يس: ٣٩]، والجسم لا يوصف بهذا القدم في أول زمان حدوثه بل بعده فقد تجدد له القدم بعدما لم يكن فيكون موجوداً زائداً على الذات فكذا القدم الذي هو التقدم بلا نهاية لا بمجرد مدة متطاولة بإبطاله أي مر مع إبطاله، فلا حاجة إلى إعادة شيء منهما وحمل ما مر على الوجهين السابقين مما لا وجه لصحته والذي يخصصه أي يختص بإبطاله أنه إن أراد به أي بالقدم أنه لا أول له فسلبي فلا يتصور كونه وجوديا أو أنه صفة لأجلها لا يختص الباري سبحانه وتعالى بحيز كما فسره أي كما فسر كلام ابن سعيد بذلك الشيخ أبو إسحاق الإسفرايني فإنه قال : معنى كلامه أنه تعالى مختص بمعنى لأجله ثبت وجوده لا في حيز كما أن المتحيز يختص بمعنى لأجله كان متحيزاً، ولا يخفى عليك أن هذا التفسير بعيد جداً عن دلالة زيادة القدم عليه. قوله : ( كالعرجون القديم العرجون عود العرق ما بين شماريخه إلى منبته من النخلة. قوله : ( فكذا القدم الذي هو التقدم بلا نهاية فيه منع للقطع بتغاير المفهومين ودليل الوجودية غير قائم لنوع أحدهما ولو سلم الاتحاد في النوع فوجودية فرد من نوع لا يستلزم وجودية فرد آخر. قوله : ( مما لا وجه لصحته لأنهما للنافين لا للمثبتين . القدم أمراً سلبياً إذ مرجعه حينئذ إلى وجوده لا في حيز ، فإن قلت : هذا السلبي معلل بالقدم لا نفسه قلت : إن الصفات السلبية لا تعلل بخلاف الثبوتية أو غيرها من المعاني فالتصوير أي فعليه تصوير ذلك المعنى المراد أولا ثم التقرير والتحقيق بإقامة الدليل عليه ثانياً هذا الذي أوردناه هاهنا في إبطاله منضم إلى ما سبق في مباحث الأمور العامة من أنه أي القدم أمر اعتباري لا وجود له في الخارج، فإنه يدل على بطلان مذهبه ودليله أيضاً . قوله : (إذ مرجعه حينئذ إلى وجوده لا في حيز في العبارة مساهلة والمقصود أن مرجعه انتفاء التحيز فلا يرد أن الوجود ليس بسلبي . قوله : قلت : إن الصفات السلبية لا تعلل أي لا تعلل بالوجودي فيكون هذا السلبي أو سلبي آخر نفس القدم لا معلوله فلا يرد تعليل عدم التحيز بالتجرد والحاجة بالإمكان .
The second attribute: pre-eternity (al-qidam). And the majority? are agreed that He is pre-eternal by Himself, not by an existential pre-eternity additional to His essence. Ibn Sa'id from the Ash'aris affirmed it, and his proof that it is an existent additional attribute is what has passed in the discussion of abiding. Its conceptualization here is that it is said: 'pre-eternal' may be applied to that which precedes in existence when time has extended over it, and from that is His statement: 'Like the old palm stalk' (Q 36:39). And a body is not described with this pre-eternity in the first time of its occurrence, but after it, so pre-eternity becomes renewed for it after it was not, so it becomes something existent additional to the essence. Likewise, pre-eternity which is temporal precedence without end is not merely an extended duration. With its refutation, meaning it has passed with its refutation, so there is no need to repeat anything from them, and to apply what has passed to the two previous aspects has no basis for its correctness. And that which specifically pertains to its refutation is that if by pre-eternity he intends that it has no beginning, then it is a negative [attribute] and cannot be conceptualized as existential, or that it is an attribute by which the Exalted is not specifically restricted to a place, as interpreted by Shaykh Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, who said: the meaning of his statement is that the Exalted is specified by a meaning by virtue of which His existence is established not in a place, just as a body (al-mutahayyiz) is specified by a meaning by virtue of which it is spatial. It is not hidden from you that this interpretation is very far from the indication of the increase of pre-eternity upon Him. His saying: 'Like the old palm stalk' – the old palm stalk is the stem of the bunch from its spadices to its source from the palm tree. His saying: 'Likewise, pre-eternity which is temporal precedence without end' – in it is a prevention of the categorical judgment that the two concepts are different, and the proof of existentiality is not established for either type, and even if unity in type is granted, the existentiality of one individual of a type does not necessitate the existentiality of another individual. His saying: 'Which has no basis for its correctness' because they are for the negators, not for the affirmers. Pre-eternity is a negative [attribute], because its reference then is to His existence not in a place. If you say: this negative is caused by pre-eternity, not itself, I say: negative attributes are not caused, unlike positive ones. So the conceptualization means that one must first conceptualize that intended meaning, then the verification by establishing the proof upon it secondly. This which we have brought here in its refutation is added to what preceded in the discussions of general matters, namely that pre-eternity is a mental consideration (iʿtibārī) having no existence in the external, for it indicates the invalidity of his doctrine and his proof as well. His saying: 'Because its reference then is to His existence not in a place' – in the expression there is leniency, and the intended meaning is that its reference is the negation of spatiality, so it is not objected that existence is not negative. His saying: 'I say: negative attributes are not caused' – meaning they are not caused by an existential [attribute], so this negative or another negative is itself pre-eternity, not its effect, so it is not objected that the negation of spatiality is caused by incorporeality (al-tajarrud) and the need by possibility.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is a sophisticated argument about the divine attribute of pre-eternity (al-qidam). It begins by stating the consensus that God is pre-eternal by Himself, not by an additional attribute of pre-eternity. Then it presents and refutes the view of Ibn Sa'id al-Ash'ari, who claimed pre-eternity is an additional attribute. The refutation takes two main lines: 1) If pre-eternity means 'no beginning,' it is a negative concept, not positive. 2) Even if interpreted as an attribute that excludes spatiality, as al-Isfarayini claimed, it is still not an additional positive attribute. The commentator then discusses the linguistic meaning of 'qidam' in the verse, distinguishing it from the theological term. The argument includes a detailed analysis of the logical consequences: if pre-eternity were an additional attribute, it would lead to an infinite regress or circularity. Ultimately, the passage concludes that pre-eternity is a mental consideration (i'tibārī) with no external existence, and this refutes Ibn Sa'id's position.

Key Terms
قدم (qidam)
وجودي (wujūdī)
سلبي (salbī)
متحيز (mutahayyiz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That pre-eternity is an additional positive attribute like other attributes of God.
Correction: The passage clarifies that pre-eternity is either a negative attribute (no beginning) or a mental consideration, not a real positive attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary argument against Ibn Sa'id's view that pre-eternity is an additional attribute?
A) It leads to circularity.
B) It is a negative concept.
C) It is a mental consideration.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
Pre-eternity is either a negative attribute (no beginning) or a mental consideration, not an additional positive attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you declare that God is pre-eternal, do you truly feel the weight of His having no beginning? How does that reality shape your daily sense of accountability to Him?
?
The passage warns against adding mental attributes to God. Are there any concepts or qualities you habitually associate with God that are not rooted in revelation? What would it take to purge them from your heart?
?
If pre-eternity is merely a negation, does that diminish your reverence for God? Or does it deepen it by reminding you that His reality transcends all positive categories we can imagine? Ponder this for your next prayer.

Divine Attributes
intermediate

Pre-eternity as a Mental Consideration (al-I'tibārī)
Concludes that pre-eternity is a mental consideration and refutes additional existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how pre-eternity is a mental consideration (i'tibārī) with no external existence, and how this refutes the view of it being an additional attribute.

Core Idea
Pre-eternity exists only in the mind, not in reality, so it cannot be a real attribute added to God.

The discussion continues with a detailed analysis of the verse 'Like the old palm stalk' to distinguish the linguistic meaning of 'qidam' from the theological. The author then concludes that pre-eternity is a mental consideration (i'tibārī), and this final point seals the refutation of Ibn Sa'id's view.

Guided Path
1
Examine the verse analysis
Read the passage where the author critiques Ibn Sa'id's use of the verse 'Like the old palm stalk' to prove pre-eternity is additional. Note the distinction between temporal precedence and eternal pre-eternity.
The author argues the two concepts are different, so the verse does not support additional pre-eternity.
2
Understand the argument that pre-eternity is negative or mental
The author clarifies that pre-eternity refers to existence not in a place, which is a negative attribute, and negative attributes are not caused.
This ties back to the earlier refutation.
3
Grasp the i'tibārī conclusion
The author states that pre-eternity is a mental consideration with no external existence, adding to the general discussions in the work.
This is the final refutation: it is not a real attribute at all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصفة الثانية : القدم وإحالة الجمهور متفقين على أنه قديم بنفسه لا بقدم وجودي زائد على ذاته، وأثبته ابن سعيد من الأشاعرة ودليله على كونه صفة موجودة زائدة ما مر في البقاء، وتصويره هاهنا أن يقال : القديم قد يطلق على المتقدم بالوجود إذا تطاول عليه الأمد، ومنه قوله تعالى : {كالعرجون القديم} [يس: ٣٩]، والجسم لا يوصف بهذا القدم في أول زمان حدوثه بل بعده فقد تجدد له القدم بعدما لم يكن فيكون موجوداً زائداً على الذات فكذا القدم الذي هو التقدم بلا نهاية لا بمجرد مدة متطاولة بإبطاله أي مر مع إبطاله، فلا حاجة إلى إعادة شيء منهما وحمل ما مر على الوجهين السابقين مما لا وجه لصحته والذي يخصصه أي يختص بإبطاله أنه إن أراد به أي بالقدم أنه لا أول له فسلبي فلا يتصور كونه وجوديا أو أنه صفة لأجلها لا يختص الباري سبحانه وتعالى بحيز كما فسره أي كما فسر كلام ابن سعيد بذلك الشيخ أبو إسحاق الإسفرايني فإنه قال : معنى كلامه أنه تعالى مختص بمعنى لأجله ثبت وجوده لا في حيز كما أن المتحيز يختص بمعنى لأجله كان متحيزاً، ولا يخفى عليك أن هذا التفسير بعيد جداً عن دلالة زيادة القدم عليه. قوله : ( كالعرجون القديم العرجون عود العرق ما بين شماريخه إلى منبته من النخلة. قوله : ( فكذا القدم الذي هو التقدم بلا نهاية فيه منع للقطع بتغاير المفهومين ودليل الوجودية غير قائم لنوع أحدهما ولو سلم الاتحاد في النوع فوجودية فرد من نوع لا يستلزم وجودية فرد آخر. قوله : ( مما لا وجه لصحته لأنهما للنافين لا للمثبتين . القدم أمراً سلبياً إذ مرجعه حينئذ إلى وجوده لا في حيز ، فإن قلت : هذا السلبي معلل بالقدم لا نفسه قلت : إن الصفات السلبية لا تعلل بخلاف الثبوتية أو غيرها من المعاني فالتصوير أي فعليه تصوير ذلك المعنى المراد أولا ثم التقرير والتحقيق بإقامة الدليل عليه ثانياً هذا الذي أوردناه هاهنا في إبطاله منضم إلى ما سبق في مباحث الأمور العامة من أنه أي القدم أمر اعتباري لا وجود له في الخارج، فإنه يدل على بطلان مذهبه ودليله أيضاً . قوله : (إذ مرجعه حينئذ إلى وجوده لا في حيز في العبارة مساهلة والمقصود أن مرجعه انتفاء التحيز فلا يرد أن الوجود ليس بسلبي . قوله : قلت : إن الصفات السلبية لا تعلل أي لا تعلل بالوجودي فيكون هذا السلبي أو سلبي آخر نفس القدم لا معلوله فلا يرد تعليل عدم التحيز بالتجرد والحاجة بالإمكان .
The second attribute: pre-eternity (al-qidam). And the majority? are agreed that He is pre-eternal by Himself, not by an existential pre-eternity additional to His essence. Ibn Sa'id from the Ash'aris affirmed it, and his proof that it is an existent additional attribute is what has passed in the discussion of abiding. Its conceptualization here is that it is said: 'pre-eternal' may be applied to that which precedes in existence when time has extended over it, and from that is His statement: 'Like the old palm stalk' (Q 36:39). And a body is not described with this pre-eternity in the first time of its occurrence, but after it, so pre-eternity becomes renewed for it after it was not, so it becomes something existent additional to the essence. Likewise, pre-eternity which is temporal precedence without end is not merely an extended duration. With its refutation, meaning it has passed with its refutation, so there is no need to repeat anything from them, and to apply what has passed to the two previous aspects has no basis for its correctness. And that which specifically pertains to its refutation is that if by pre-eternity he intends that it has no beginning, then it is a negative [attribute] and cannot be conceptualized as existential, or that it is an attribute by which the Exalted is not specifically restricted to a place, as interpreted by Shaykh Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, who said: the meaning of his statement is that the Exalted is specified by a meaning by virtue of which His existence is established not in a place, just as a body (al-mutahayyiz) is specified by a meaning by virtue of which it is spatial. It is not hidden from you that this interpretation is very far from the indication of the increase of pre-eternity upon Him. His saying: 'Like the old palm stalk' – the old palm stalk is the stem of the bunch from its spadices to its source from the palm tree. His saying: 'Likewise, pre-eternity which is temporal precedence without end' – in it is a prevention of the categorical judgment that the two concepts are different, and the proof of existentiality is not established for either type, and even if unity in type is granted, the existentiality of one individual of a type does not necessitate the existentiality of another individual. His saying: 'Which has no basis for its correctness' because they are for the negators, not for the affirmers. Pre-eternity is a negative [attribute], because its reference then is to His existence not in a place. If you say: this negative is caused by pre-eternity, not itself, I say: negative attributes are not caused, unlike positive ones. So the conceptualization means that one must first conceptualize that intended meaning, then the verification by establishing the proof upon it secondly. This which we have brought here in its refutation is added to what preceded in the discussions of general matters, namely that pre-eternity is a mental consideration (iʿtibārī) having no existence in the external, for it indicates the invalidity of his doctrine and his proof as well. His saying: 'Because its reference then is to His existence not in a place' – in the expression there is leniency, and the intended meaning is that its reference is the negation of spatiality, so it is not objected that existence is not negative. His saying: 'I say: negative attributes are not caused' – meaning they are not caused by an existential [attribute], so this negative or another negative is itself pre-eternity, not its effect, so it is not objected that the negation of spatiality is caused by incorporeality (al-tajarrud) and the need by possibility.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is a sophisticated argument about the divine attribute of pre-eternity (al-qidam). It begins by stating the consensus that God is pre-eternal by Himself, not by an additional attribute of pre-eternity. Then it presents and refutes the view of Ibn Sa'id al-Ash'ari, who claimed pre-eternity is an additional attribute. The refutation takes two main lines: 1) If pre-eternity means 'no beginning,' it is a negative concept, not positive. 2) Even if interpreted as an attribute that excludes spatiality, as al-Isfarayini claimed, it is still not an additional positive attribute. The commentator then discusses the linguistic meaning of 'qidam' in the verse, distinguishing it from the theological term. The argument includes a detailed analysis of the logical consequences: if pre-eternity were an additional attribute, it would lead to an infinite regress or circularity. Ultimately, the passage concludes that pre-eternity is a mental consideration (i'tibārī) with no external existence, and this refutes Ibn Sa'id's position.

Key Terms
قدم (qidam)
سلبي (salbī)
اعتباري (iʿtibārī)
علة (ʿilla)
معلول (maʿlūl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That pre-eternity is an additional positive attribute that exists externally.
Correction: The passage demonstrates that pre-eternity is a mental consideration (i'tibārī) and a negative attribute, not an external positive reality.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude about pre-eternity (al-qidam) in the final part of the passage?
A) It is a positive attribute.
B) It is a negative attribute.
C) It is a mental consideration (iʿtibārī).
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
Pre-eternity is both a negative attribute and a mental consideration, not an external positive attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

In what specific way does the lesson's claim that it is a mental consideration challenge your default mode of thought? ?
The passage argues that negative attributes are not caused. Have you ever treated God's attributes as if they were caused—perhaps by His essence or by each other? How might such a subtle assumption detract from your worship of God as absolutely simple and uncaused? Choose one attribute you have been thinking of in causal terms and correct it in your heart today.

Are you willing to admit that your most cherished theological ideas may be only mental tools, not perfect descriptions of His reality? What is one concept about God you are holding onto that might be just a mental construct, and will you let it go for the sake of knowing Him as He is?
